<meta name="DC.Description" content="While evolution has been thought of as playing out over millions of years, adaptation to new environments can occur very rapidly, presenting us with key opportunities to understand evolutionary dynamics. One of the most amazing examples of real-time evolution comes from agriculture, where due to the intense use of a few herbicides, many plant species have evolved herbicide resistance to become aggressive weeds. An important question has been whether herbicide resistance arises only rarely and then spreads quickly, or whether herbicide resistance arises all the time de novo. Our work with glyphosate resistance in US Midwestern and Canadian populations of Amaranthus tuberculatus reveals the answer to be, “it depends,” as we surprisingly find examples for both modes of evolution." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Multiple modes of convergent adaptation in the spread of glyphosate-resistant Amaranthus tuberculatus" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;The selection pressure exerted by herbicides has led to the repeated evolution of herbicide resistance in weeds. The evolution of herbicide resistance on contemporary timescales in turn provides an outstanding opportunity to investigate key questions about the genetics of adaptation, in particular the relative importance of adaptation from new mutations, standing genetic variation, or geographic spread of adaptive alleles through gene flow. Glyphosate-resistant &lt;i&gt;Amaranthus tuberculatus&lt;/i&gt; poses one of the most significant threats to crop yields in the Midwestern United States, with both agricultural populations and herbicide resistance only recently emerging in Canada. To understand the evolutionary mechanisms driving the spread of resistance, we sequenced and assembled the &lt;i&gt;A. tuberculatus&lt;/i&gt; genome and investigated the origins and population genomics of 163 resequenced glyphosate-resistant and susceptible individuals from Canada and the United States. In Canada, we discovered multiple modes of convergent evolution: in one locality, resistance appears to have evolved through introductions of preadapted US genotypes, while in another, there is evidence for the independent evolution of resistance on genomic backgrounds that are historically nonagricultural. Moreover, resistance on these local, nonagricultural backgrounds appears to have occurred predominantly through the partial sweep of a single haplotype. In contrast, resistant haplotypes arising from the Midwestern United States show multiple amplification haplotypes segregating both between and within populations. Therefore, while the remarkable species-wide diversity of &lt;i&gt;A. tuberculatus&lt;/i&gt; has facilitated geographic parallel adaptation of glyphosate resistance, more recently established agricultural populations are limited to adaptation in a more mutation-limited framework.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;While evolution has been thought of as playing out over millions of years, adaptation to new environments can occur very rapidly, presenting us with key opportunities to understand evolutionary dynamics. One of the most amazing examples of real-time evolution comes from agriculture, where due to the intense use of a few herbicides, many plant species have evolved herbicide resistance to become aggressive weeds. An important question has been whether herbicide resistance arises only rarely and then spreads quickly, or whether herbicide resistance arises all the time de novo. Our work with glyphosate resistance in US Midwestern and Canadian populations of &lt;i&gt;Amaranthus tuberculatus&lt;/i&gt; reveals the answer to be, “it depends,” as we surprisingly find examples for both modes of evolution.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Multiple modes of convergent adaptation in the spread of glyphosate-resistant Amaranthus tuberculatus" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/116/42/21076/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="While evolution has been thought of as playing out over millions of years, adaptation to new environments can occur very rapidly, presenting us with key opportunities to understand evolutionary dynamics. One of the most amazing examples of real-time evolution comes from agriculture, where due to the intense use of a few herbicides, many plant species have evolved herbicide resistance to become aggressive weeds. An important question has been whether herbicide resistance arises only rarely and then spreads quickly, or whether herbicide resistance arises all the time de novo. Our work with glyphosate resistance in US Midwestern and Canadian populations of Amaranthus tuberculatus reveals the answer to be, “it depends,” as we surprisingly find examples for both modes of evolution." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="While evolution has been thought of as playing out over millions of years, adaptation to new environments can occur very rapidly, presenting us with key opportunities to understand evolutionary dynamics. One of the most amazing examples of real-time evolution comes from agriculture, where due to the intense use of a few herbicides, many plant species have evolved herbicide resistance to become aggressive weeds. An important question has been whether herbicide resistance arises only rarely and then spreads quickly, or whether herbicide resistance arises all the time de novo. Our work with glyphosate resistance in US Midwestern and Canadian populations of Amaranthus tuberculatus reveals the answer to be, “it depends,” as we surprisingly find examples for both modes of evolution." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="893139" id="node893139" data-pisa="pnas;116/42/21076" data-pisa-master="pnas;1900870116" data-apath="/pnas/116/42/21076.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">

      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Multiple modes of convergent adaptation in the spread of glyphosate-resistant <em>Amaranthus tuberculatus</em></h1>

Kreiner</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Darci Ann Giacomini</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="2"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6557-4898" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Felix Bemm</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Bridgit Waithaka</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="4">Julian Regalado</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Christa Lanz</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="6">Julia Hildebrandt</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="7">Peter H. <div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-6">While evolution has been thought of as playing out over millions of years, adaptation to new environments can occur very rapidly, presenting us with key opportunities to understand evolutionary dynamics. One of the most amazing examples of real-time evolution comes from agriculture, where due to the intense use of a few herbicides, many plant species have evolved herbicide resistance to become aggressive weeds. An important question has been whether herbicide resistance arises only rarely and then spreads quickly, or whether herbicide resistance arises all the time de novo. Our work with glyphosate resistance in US Midwestern and Canadian populations of <em>Amaranthus tuberculatus</em> reveals the answer to be, “it depends,” as we surprisingly find examples for both modes of evolution.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7">The selection pressure exerted by herbicides has led to the repeated evolution of herbicide resistance in weeds. The evolution of herbicide resistance on contemporary timescales in turn provides an outstanding opportunity to investigate key questions about the genetics of adaptation, in particular the relative importance of adaptation from new mutations, standing genetic variation, or geographic spread of adaptive alleles through gene flow. Glyphosate-resistant <em>Amaranthus tuberculatus</em> poses one of the most significant threats to crop yields in the Midwestern United States, with both agricultural populations and herbicide resistance only recently emerging in Canada. To understand the evolutionary mechanisms driving the spread of resistance, we sequenced and assembled the <em>A. tuberculatus</em> genome and investigated the origins and population genomics of 163 resequenced glyphosate-resistant and susceptible individuals from Canada and the United States. In Canada, we discovered multiple modes of convergent evolution: in one locality, resistance appears to have evolved through introductions of preadapted US genotypes, while in another, there is evidence for the independent evolution of resistance on genomic backgrounds that are historically nonagricultural. Moreover, resistance on these local, nonagricultural backgrounds appears to have occurred predominantly through the partial sweep of a single haplotype. In contrast, resistant haplotypes arising from the Midwestern United States show multiple amplification haplotypes segregating both between and within populations. Therefore, while the remarkable species-wide diversity of <em>A. tuberculatus</em> has facilitated geographic parallel adaptation of glyphosate resistance, more recently established agricultural populations are limited to adaptation in a more mutation-limited framework.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/parallel-evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-parallel-evolution" rel="nofollow">parallel evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/herbicide-resistance" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-herbicide-resistance" rel="nofollow">herbicide resistance</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/de-novo-mutation" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-de-novo-mutation" rel="nofollow">de novo mutation</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/gene-flow" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-gene-flow" rel="nofollow">gene flow</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/population-genomics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-population-genomics" rel="nofollow">population genomics</a></li></ul><p id="p-8" class="flushleft">Glyphosate-resistant <em>Amaranthus tuberculatus</em> was first reported in Missouri in 2005 but has since been reported in 19 US states (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>), with resistant biotypes harming corn and soybean yields (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Resistance to glyphosate in weed populations is widespread, likely as a result of the rapid adoption of and reliance on glyphosate weed control technology; 84% of soybeans, 60% of cotton, and 20% of corn grown in the United States by 2004 carried transgenes for glyphosate resistance, despite Roundup Ready technology—the combination of glyphosate weed control with transgenic glyphosate resistance—only having been introduced 8 y earlier (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). Agriculturally associated <em>A. tuberculatus</em> weed populations emerged in Canada in the province of Ontario only in the early 2000s, with glyphosate resistance following a decade later (<a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). As with other herbicides, resistance in weed populations can evolve via substitutions at the direct target of glyphosate, 5-enolpyruvylshikimate-3-phosphate synthase (EPSPS), or by polygenic adaptation involving different loci in the genome (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a><a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-7"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-8"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-9"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). More often, glyphosate resistance in the genus <em>Amaranthus</em> has an unusual genetic basis: amplification of the <em>EPSPS</em> locus (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a><a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-12"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-13"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-14"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). Gene amplification apparently evolved independently in 2 <em>Amaranthus</em> species (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>, <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>, <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>), raising the possibility that it could have evolved multiple times independently within a single species, or even population (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>). While glyphosate resistance has been studied from multiple angles (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>, <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a><a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-20"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-21"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-22"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>), the recent discovery of glyphosate-resistant <em>A. tuberculatus</em> in southwestern Ontario affords the unique opportunity to evaluate the evolutionary origins of herbicide resistance, whether it has arisen through de novo mutation or standing genetic variation, and the role of gene flow in the recent spread of herbicide resistance in an agronomically important weed.</p><p id="p-9">Native to North America, the dioecious, wind-pollinated <em>A. tuberculatus</em> has a history marked by the interaction of 2 lineages or subspecies [sensu Costea and Tardif (<a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>) and Pratt and Clark (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>)], thought to have been diverging on either side of the Mississippi River until they were brought back into contact through human-mediated disturbance (<a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>, <a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). Morphological, herbarium, and microsatellite evidence point to an expansion of the western var. <em>rudis</em> subspecies range limits over the last 50 y, while the range of more eastern var. <em>tuberculatus</em> subspecies is thought to be stagnant and constrained to riparian habitats (<a id="xref-ref-22-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>, <a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>). With the timing of the var. <em>rudis</em> expansion coinciding with the invasion of <em>A. tuberculatus</em> into agricultural environments, var. <em>rudis</em> is hypothesized to be a predominant driver of this agricultural invasion (<a id="xref-ref-23-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>).</p><div class="section" id="sec-1"><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Sequencing and Collections.</h3><p id="p-10">We assembled a high-quality reference genome for <em>A. tuberculatus</em> from a single individual from 58 Gb (∼87× genome coverage) long-read data collected on the Pacific Biosciences Sequel platform using 15 SMRT cells. After assembly, polishing, and haplotype merging, the reference genome consisted of 2,514 contigs with a total size of 663 Mb and an N50 of 1.7 Mb (see <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>, for details). Our final genome size is consistent with recent cytometric estimates of 676 Mb (SE = 27 Mb) for <em>A. tuberculatus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>). The new reference included 88% of the near-universal single-copy orthologs present in BUSCO’s Embryophyta benchmarking dataset, with 6% marked as duplicates (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>). For chromosome-scale sweep scan analyses, we further scaffolded our contigs onto the fully resolved <em>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</em> genome (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>), resulting in 16 final pseudomolecules (which included 99.8% of our original assembly; <a id="xref-sec-8-1" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Methods</em></a>) for population genetic analyses.</p><p id="p-11">We resequenced whole genomes of 163 individuals to about 10× coverage from 19 agricultural fields in Missouri, Illinois, and 2 regions where glyphosate resistance has recently appeared in Ontario: Essex County, an agriculturally important region in southwestern Ontario; and Walpole Island, an expansive wetland and First Nation reserve with growing agricultural activity (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>). Populations from the Midwest (Missouri and Illinois) had been previously assayed for glyphosate-resistant phenotypes, and from qPCR of genomic DNA it was found that resistance was predominantly conferred through <em>EPSPS</em> copy number amplification (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Populations from Walpole Island and Essex County in Ontario were sampled in 2016 after reports from farmers that they were not controlled by herbicides. We also sampled 10 individuals from riparian habitats in Ontario near Walpole Island and Essex County, as a nonagricultural, natural Canadian comparison (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>). Genome-wide diversity in <em>A. tuberculatus</em> is quite high, even relative to other wind-pollinated outcrossers (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>), with neutral diversity (mean pairwise difference) at 4-fold degenerate sites being 0.041. The frequency of glyphosate resistance in the sampled agricultural fields ranged from 13 to 88%, based on greenhouse trials (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a> and <a id="xref-sec-8-2" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Methods</em></a>). Plants from natural populations in Ontario had no glyphosate resistance.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Population structure and demographic history in Amaranthus tuberculatus. </ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-12" class="first-child">Population structure and demographic history in <em>Amaranthus tuberculatus.</em> (<em>A</em>) Demographic model of the 2 <em>A. tuberculatus</em> subspecies (<em>N</em><sub>a</sub> = ancestral effective population size, with migration and other effective population size estimates scaled accordingly). (<em>C</em>) STRUCTURE plot of admixture across regions and populations from west (<em>Left</em>) to east (<em>Right</em>), with most likely number of clusters (<em>K</em>) = 3.
