(<em>E</em>) PCA of all individuals, with both PC1 and PC2 significantly relating to both longitude and latitude (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Demography of <em>Amaranthus tuberculatus</em>.</h3><p id="p-13">To dissect the demographic context of convergent adaptation to glyphosate, we characterized genome-wide patterns of population structure, demography, and differentiation. Population structure, demographic modeling (<a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>), and phenotypic characterization confirmed the presence of the 2 previously hypothesized ancestral lineages, <em>A. tuberculatus</em> var. <em>rudis</em> and <em>A. tuberculatus</em> var. <em>tuberculatus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-22-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>, <a id="xref-ref-23-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>). Population structure and investigations of the genome-wide proportion of introgression [<em>f</em> statistic (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>)] largely reflected previous accounts of the historical range limits (<a id="xref-ref-22-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>): Natural Ontario populations had the diagnostic indehiscent seed phenotype and were genetically homogeneous for ancestry of the var. <em>tuberculatus</em> lineage; Missouri samples were homogeneous for the var. <em>rudis</em> lineage; while Illinois, a region of sympatry in the historical range of the 2 subspecies, showed signs of introgression from var. <em>tuberculatus</em> {mixture proportion, <em>f</em> [95% confidence interval (CI)] = 0.1342 [0.126, 0.143], using Missouri as a reference (<a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>, <a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>)} (<a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1 <em>B</em> and <em>C</em></a>). Genetic differentiation (F<sub>ST</sub>) between individuals with ancestry homogenous for different lineages at <em>K</em> = 2 was 0.212, on par with or greater than that between other congeners (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>). Moreover, both longitude and latitude significantly explained both PC1 and PC2 of the SNP matrix (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>), with PC1 separating var. <em>rudis</em> and var. <em>tuberculatus</em> ancestry, and PC2 separating Canadian and American accessions (<a id="xref-fig-1-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>E</em></a>). These patterns of diversity resulted in principal-component representation that, with few exceptions, reflected the geography of our samples. The most likely <em>tuberculatus-rudis</em> demographic model was one of secondary contact, with var. <em>rudis</em> having undergone a bottleneck followed by a dramatic expansion, which may be indicative of this subspecies’ rapid colonization of agricultural fields across North America (<a id="xref-fig-1-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Demographic Origins of Canadian Agricultural Populations.</h3><p id="p-14">Analyses of newly problematic agricultural populations in Ontario provides a unique angle for tracking the demographic source of the <em>A. tuberculatus</em> agricultural invasion. Populations from Essex County fell completely within the var. <em>rudis</em> cluster, with a <em>treemix</em> model indicating that Essex populations were derived from the most western Missouri population (<a id="xref-fig-1-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>E</em></a>), the source of almost the entire Essex genome (<em>f</em>[95% CI] = 0.996 [0.985, 1]). Furthermore, while Essex grouped with Walpole and Natural populations on PC2, it was found at the other end on PC1, more different from Canadian populations than even the most geographically distant Missouri population (<a id="xref-fig-1-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>). These patterns of population structure were distinct from the continuous gradient of southwest–northeast ancestry previously reported (<a id="xref-ref-23-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>) and supports the hypothesis that glyphosate-resistant <em>A. tuberculatus</em> was introduced to Ontario from the United States through seed-contaminated agricultural machinery (<a id="xref-ref-3-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>) or animal-mediated seed dispersal (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>).</p><p id="p-15">In contrast to Essex as a likely introduction of a preadapted genotype to a new locale, populations from Walpole Island, where glyphosate resistance was first reported in Ontario (<a id="xref-ref-5-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>), were mainly of the native, eastern var. <em>tuberculatus</em> type (<a id="xref-fig-1-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). However, the convergent evolution of var. <em>tuberculatus</em> into agricultural fields may not be solely the result of de novo mutations. Populations from Walpole Island showed signs of introgression from var. <em>rudis</em> (<em>f</em>[95% CI] = 0.225 [0.215, 0.236]), while <em>treemix</em> indicated that Walpole may be a hot spot for gene flow, with 9 of 10 total migration events across the tree involving Walpole (explaining an additional 2.5% of SNP variation compared to a migration-free model) (<a id="xref-fig-1-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>C</em></a>). Thus, both adaptive introgression from the western var. <em>rudis</em> clade and/or de novo adaptation from local natural populations could be playing a role in the evolution of resistance and adaptation to agricultural environments in Walpole.</p><p id="p-16">Despite the considerable level of var. <em>rudis</em> introgression into Walpole, these populations were similarly differentiated from nearby natural populations homogenous for var. <em>tuberculatus</em> ancestry as they were from comparably admixed populations in Illinois [F<sub>ST</sub> <sub>(Walpole-Nat)</sub> = 0.0286; F<sub>ST</sub> <sub>(Walpole-Illinois)</sub> = 0.0284; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>]. This, along with the tight clustering of Walpole and Natural populations in the PCA and structure analyses, implies that Walpole populations experienced strong and rapid local adaptation to agricultural environments upon its conversion from wetland, which may have been facilitated by introgression from var. <em>rudis</em>. We therefore sought to find genes that were highly differentiated between Walpole and Natural populations, putatively involved in agricultural adaptation. A Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment test for the top 1% of genes with excess differentiation between Walpole and natural populations identified genes with monooxygenase and oxidoreductase molecular function, with histone methylation biological function, and several protein classes involved in transport and amino acid/protein modification (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>). Of particular interest were 2 enriched GO categories important in metabolic, nontarget site resistance: oxidoreductases, which include cytochrome P450s, and peptidyl-amino acid modifiers, which include glycosyltransferases (<a id="xref-ref-7-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). CYP450s and glycosyltransferases work consecutively to detoxify herbicides in plant cells by catalyzing hydroxylation and glc-conjugation (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). In addition to these 2 gene families being highly differentiated between Walpole and Natural populations, we also investigated the possibility for copy number expansion or contraction. An analysis of the median copy number of 100-bp windows within each gene, for each individual (<a id="xref-sec-8-3" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Methods</em></a>), revealed that genes in both gene families consistently had significantly expanded copy number in Walpole populations (least square means: CYP450s, 1.82 [95% CI, 1.80, 1.85]; glycosyltransferases, 2.04 [95% CI, 2.02, 2.06]) compared to natural populations (least square means: CYP450s, 1.67 [95% CI, 1.62, 1.71]; glycosyltransferases, 1.83 [95% CI, 1.79, 1.86]) (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>). Despite this widespread pattern across 69 CYP450 and 64 glycosyltransferase genes, no CYP450s or glycosyltransferases were significantly correlated with our phenotypic rating of glyphosate resistance after Holm’s correction for multiple testing. A possible explanation is that the copy number expansion of these gene families confers resistance to herbicides other than just glyphosate, or, more broadly, is a result of the transition from natural to agricultural habitats.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Enrichment and expansion of CYP450 and glycosyltransferase gene families in the transition from natural to agricultural in Walpole. (A) GO categories that were significantly enriched in an analysis of the 99th percentile of Fst outliers between Walpole and Natural populations. Light gray indicates GO categories that include CYP450s; dark gray indicates the category that includes glycosyltransferases. (B) Evidence for copy number expansion of both CYP450 and glycosyltransferase genes in Walpole relative to Natural populations. Each row represents a single annotated gene of a given gene family, with each density plot representing the distribution of the median copy number, inferred from 100-bp windows, across individuals for that gene." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1654348770" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Enrichment and expansion of CYP450 and glycosyltransferase gene families in the transition from natural to agricultural in Walpole. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) GO categories that were significantly enriched in an analysis of the 99th percentile of F&lt;sub&gt;st&lt;/sub&gt; outliers between Walpole and Natural populations. Light gray indicates GO categories that include CYP450s; dark gray indicates the category that includes glycosyltransferases. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Evidence for copy number expansion of both CYP450 and glycosyltransferase genes in Walpole relative to Natural populations. Each row represents a single annotated gene of a given gene family, with each density plot representing the distribution of the median copy number, inferred from 100-bp windows, across individuals for that gene.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F2.medium.gif" width="275" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F2.medium.gif" width="275" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/893284" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-17" class="first-child">Enrichment and expansion of CYP450 and glycosyltransferase gene families in the transition from natural to agricultural in Walpole. (<em>A</em>) GO categories that were significantly enriched in an analysis of the 99th percentile of F<sub>st</sub> outliers between Walpole and Natural populations. Light gray indicates GO categories that include CYP450s; dark gray indicates the category that includes glycosyltransferases. (<em>B</em>) Evidence for copy number expansion of both CYP450 and glycosyltransferase genes in Walpole relative to Natural populations. Each row represents a single annotated gene of a given gene family, with each density plot representing the distribution of the median copy number, inferred from 100-bp windows, across individuals for that gene.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Genetic Mechanisms of Glyphosate Resistance.</h3><p id="p-18">Two major evolutionary paths to glyphosate resistance are amplification of wild-type <em>EPSPS</em> and nonsynonymous mutations in <em>EPSPS</em> that make the enzyme resistant to glyphosate inhibition. To better understand the genetic mechanisms underpinning glyphosate resistance, we investigated how variation in resistance relates to these 2 classes of <em>EPSPS</em> mutations. Using our genomic data to quantify copy number (<a id="xref-sec-8-4" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Methods</em></a>), we found that of 84 individuals assayed in the greenhouse as resistant (resistance, ≥2/5 rating; <a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>), 60 (71%) had elevated <em>EPSPS</em> copy number (&gt;1.5; as in ref. <a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). However, almost 26% (22 of 83) of individuals assayed as susceptible had an <em>EPSPS</em> copy number &gt;1.5 (compared to 15% or 13 of 88 individuals for a &gt;2 cutoff). Apart from errors in phenotyping or copy number estimation, this implies that intermediate copy number amplification alone may not always be sufficient for resistance, e.g., if amplified copies are not properly expressed. While <em>EPSPS</em> amplification was most frequent in the Midwest (83% [33 of 40] of resistant individuals, compared to 70% [16 of 23] in Walpole and 52% [11 of 21] in Essex), copy number in resistant individuals was on average almost twice as high in Walpole (∼9 copies on average, compared to 5 in the Midwest and 4 in Essex). Previous estimates of <em>EPSPS</em> copy number in resistant <em>A. tuberculatus</em> were up to 17.5 copies relative to diploid susceptibles (<a id="xref-ref-11-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>); we found 2 individuals in Walpole with an estimated 29 copies (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>). A regression of resistance onto copy number was significant in all 3 geographic regions (Walpole, <em>P</em> = 2.6e-07; Essex, <em>P</em> = 0.002; Midwest, <em>P</em> = 3.5e-06), explaining 48% of the variation in resistance in Walpole, but only 23% and 27% in Essex and the Midwest. In these latter 2 regions, however, an additional 10% of variation was explained by a nonsynonymous substitution at codon 106, the most common and well-characterized genetic mechanism of glyphosate resistance across species outside of the genus <em>Amaranthus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>), in this instance causing a change from proline to serine (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="EPSPS copy number variation among individuals, and its relationship with resistance. EPSPS copy number significantly explains phenotypic resistance within each agricultural region (solid linear regression line) (Walpole, P = 2.6e-07; Essex, P = 0.002; Midwest, P = 3.5e-06), with P106S substitution in EPSPS (red individuals) increasing explained resistance in Essex and the Midwest, but not in Walpole (dashed linear regression line)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1654348770" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; copy number variation among individuals, and its relationship with resistance. &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; copy number significantly explains phenotypic resistance within each agricultural region (solid linear regression line) (Walpole, &lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; = 2.6e-07; Essex, &lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; = 0.002; Midwest, &lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; = 3.5e-06), with P106S substitution in &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; (red individuals) increasing explained resistance in Essex and the Midwest, but not in Walpole (dashed linear regression line).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="158"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="158"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/893245" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-19" class="first-child"><em>EPSPS</em> copy number variation among individuals, and its relationship with resistance. <em>EPSPS</em> copy number significantly explains phenotypic resistance within each agricultural region (solid linear regression line) (Walpole, <em>P</em> = 2.6e-07; Essex, <em>P</em> = 0.002; Midwest, <em>P</em> = 3.5e-06), with P106S substitution in <em>EPSPS</em> (red individuals) increasing explained resistance in Essex and the Midwest, but not in Walpole (dashed linear regression line).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-20">The presence of 2 types of target-site resistance mechanisms, copy number increase and nonsynonymous mutation at a critical codon, implies parallel evolution of the resistance phenotype through independent genetic pathways. While the well-known P106S nonsynonymous mutation can account for some of the resistance unexplained by copy number increase alone, other uncharacterized nontarget site mechanisms are likely contributing as well and thus providing a further path to convergent evolutionary outcomes. In addition to shedding light on the prevalence of different resistance mechanisms, the population genomic data allowed us to determine whether our most prevalent genetic mechanism, namely the <em>EPSPS</em> gene amplification, arose multiple times.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Genetic Origins of the <em>EPSPS</em> Amplification.</h3><p id="p-21">Our chromosome-scale genome assembly provided a unique opportunity to determine the structure and genomic footprint of selection around the amplified <em>EPSPS</em> locus in different populations. Across all populations, copy number increase was not restricted to the 10-kb <em>EPSPS</em> gene—individuals identified to have increased copy number at <em>EPSPS</em> also had a correlated increase in the mean and variance of copy number for up to 6.5 Mb of the reference genome (23.5 to 30 Mb on chromosome 5), encompassing 108 genes (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2</a>).</p><p id="p-22">Characterizing signals of selection for a high copy number region can be challenging. First, typical population genetic statistics ignore potential variation among gene copies that are collapsed into a single haplotype. Ideally, phasing of a multicopy region would allow for full resolution of the single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) differences within and between haplotypes. However, very recent gene amplification is expected to limit SNP variation among amplified copies, and will also hinder phasing approaches from short read data. Second, variation may not be recognized because of allelic dropout of low-copy variants. Analysis of the relationship between EPSPS copy number and homozygosity in our dataset suggested that higher-copy haplotypes did not feature more SNP variation than lower-copy haplotypes, implying that generally few new mutations distinguish among amplified copies (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>). Nonetheless, to control for the possibility of residual SNP differences that exist among gene copies and/or for allelic dropout, we created a consensus haploid sequence by random downsampling to 1 allele per heterozygous site. Because we downsampled SNP by SNP, we do not expect our downsampling to be biased toward any particular haplotype.</p><p id="p-23">While the <em>EPSPS-</em>related amplification showed the strongest selective signal on all of chromosome 5, we found distinct selective sweep patterns in the different agricultural regions. We ran <em>Sweepfinder2</em> (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>, <a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>) across chromosome 5 to identify focal windows with a site-frequency spectrum particularly skewed by selection (while controlling for recombination) relative to genome-wide 4-fold degenerate sites. <em>Sweepfinder2</em> estimated the strongest amplification-related sweep signal in Walpole. In contrast to Essex or the Midwest, the top 1% of apparently selected 10-kb windows on chromosome 5 were localized to the amplified <em>EPSPS</em> region in Walpole (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>). Moreover, there was a marked reduction in genetic diversity (mean pairwise differences) around <em>EPSPS</em>, as well as elevated differentiation (F<sub>st</sub>) and extended haplotype homozygosity [XP-EHH score (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>)] in Walpole individuals with the <em>EPSPS</em> amplification, implying a hard selective sweep, but not in Essex or Midwest individuals with increased <em>EPSPS</em> copy number (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>).</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Population genetic signals of selection related to copy number increase around EPSPS on chromosome 5. The deficit of diversity (Top), relative differentiation (Middle), and difference in extended haplotype homozygosity [XP-EHH (34)] (Bottom) is compared between amplified and nonamplified individuals in each agricultural region. EPSPS is delimited by the vertical gray dashed line, while the EPSPS-linked region undergoing amplification is shown by the light gray box, spanning 23.5 to 30 Mb on chromosome 5." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1654348770" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Population genetic signals of selection related to copy number increase around &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; on chromosome 5. The deficit of diversity (&lt;em&gt;Top&lt;/em&gt;), relative differentiation (&lt;em&gt;Middle&lt;/em&gt;), and difference in extended haplotype homozygosity [XP-EHH (34)] (&lt;em&gt;Bottom&lt;/em&gt;) is compared between amplified and nonamplified individuals in each agricultural region. &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; is delimited by the vertical gray dashed line, while the &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt;-linked region undergoing amplification is shown by the light gray box, spanning 23.5 to 30 Mb on chromosome 5.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="181"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 4." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="181"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/893502" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 4.</span> <p id="p-24" class="first-child">Population genetic signals of selection related to copy number increase around <em>EPSPS</em> on chromosome 5. The deficit of diversity (<em>Top</em>), relative differentiation (<em>Middle</em>), and difference in extended haplotype homozygosity [XP-EHH (<a id="xref-ref-34-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>)] (<em>Bottom</em>) is compared between amplified and nonamplified individuals in each agricultural region. <em>EPSPS</em> is delimited by the vertical gray dashed line, while the <em>EPSPS</em>-linked region undergoing amplification is shown by the light gray box, spanning 23.5 to 30 Mb on chromosome 5.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-25">These differences in the extent of the amplification-related sweep signals across agricultural regions may be a consequence of how often <em>EPSPS</em> amplification has evolved; a hard sweep would be indicative of it having arisen only once, while soft sweeps would point to multiple origins (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a><a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-37"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-38"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>). To investigate this further, we mapped <em>EPSPS</em> copy number onto a maximum-likelihood haplotype tree produced from SNP variants in <em>EPSPS</em>, and compared the phylogeny with phenotypic resistance and nonsynonymous target-site resistance status (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). Indeed, the agricultural regions differed in the inferred number of independent copy number increases. Whereas there appears to have been only one amplification event in Walpole, Essex haplotypes of individuals with copy number increases are interspersed with susceptible haplotypes, both within and between populations. Similarly, haplotypes from Midwest individuals with <em>EPSPS</em> amplification are distributed across the gene tree, although some local populations show clustering indicative of a local hard sweep, implying independent evolutionary origins among populations and occasionally within populations in the Midwest (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>).</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Diversity of EPSPS amplification origins across agricultural regions. For each agricultural region, we show a haplotype tree of based on SNPs within EPSPS (with tips colored by population of origin), alongside a bar plot of EPSPS copy number, and a matrix of phenotypic resistance and target site-resistance status for the Gly-106-Pro mutation." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1654348770" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Diversity of &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; amplification origins across agricultural regions. For each agricultural region, we show a haplotype tree of based on SNPs within &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; (with tips colored by population of origin), alongside a bar plot of &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; copy number, and a matrix of phenotypic resistance and target site-resistance status for the Gly-106-Pro mutation.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="188"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 5." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="188"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/893324" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 5.</span> <p id="p-26" class="first-child">Diversity of <em>EPSPS</em> amplification origins across agricultural regions. For each agricultural region, we show a haplotype tree of based on SNPs within <em>EPSPS</em> (with tips colored by population of origin), alongside a bar plot of <em>EPSPS</em> copy number, and a matrix of phenotypic resistance and target site-resistance status for the Gly-106-Pro mutation.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-27">Together, these analyses suggest that gene amplification has occurred multiple times independently to different extents across the geographic range. However, it is possible that recombination and de novo mutation after amplification have contributed to the apparent soft sweep signal. To further test for multiple independent origins, we looked at the similarity in the copy number profiles of the <em>EPSPS</em> region, which should also be independent of any possible artifacts due to minority allele dropout in resequencing data. The copy number profiles of the amplified region varied considerably across our samples, and especially across agricultural regions (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>A</em></a>), consistent with multiple independent amplification events. To quantify this, we calculated the Spearman’s rank correlation coefficient of normalized sequence coverage in the 1-Mb chromosomal segment surrounding <em>EPSPS</em> between all possible pairs of individuals with copy number increases—this quantifies only the similarity in the rank, and not amplitude, of the landscape of copy number across loci within the segment (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>B</em></a>). In agreement with our polymorphism-based inferences, the 2 Canadian regions showed very different patterns; coverages in different Walpole individuals were very highly correlated (average of Spearman’s ρ = 0.95), suggesting the spread of a single amplification haplotype through a hard selective sweep. In contrast, there was much a lower average correlation across all Essex individuals region-wide (ρ = 0.56), and this was the case even when looking at the average within-population correlations rather than the single region-wide average (within-population average, e.g., ρ = 0.54 and 0.61), suggesting different haplotypes had independently experienced copy number increases (<a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). Similar to Essex, there appeared to be multiple amplification haplotypes in the Midwest (average for all individuals, ρ = 0.47), but within-population correlations were higher, consistent with hard (ρ = 0.94, 0.95, 0.93) or soft sweeps (ρ = 0.66, 0.74, 0.75) (<a id="xref-fig-6-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>).</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Similarity of the EPSPS amplification inferred from copy number variation around the EPSPS gene. (A) The profile of normalized sequence coverage for 1 Mb around EPSPS (locus delimited by dashed line). Color of each bar under the curve represents copy number at that genomic position, where height of each profile has been rescaled by the max value within each region for clarity (Walpole, Essex, and Midwest, separately). Only individuals with at least 1.5&#xD7; coverage relative to susceptible individuals were considered. (B) Pairwise Spearman&#x2019;s correlation coefficients over the same 1-Mb EPSPS region for individuals shown in A. Mean coefficient across all individuals within each agricultural region indicated. Order of individuals within each region as in A, from Bottom to Top and Right to Left." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1654348770" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Similarity of the &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; amplification inferred from copy number variation around the &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; gene. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) The profile of normalized sequence coverage for 1 Mb around EPSPS (locus delimited by dashed line). Color of each bar under the curve represents copy number at that genomic position, where height of each profile has been rescaled by the max value within each region for clarity (Walpole, Essex, and Midwest, separately). Only individuals with at least 1.5&#xD7; coverage relative to susceptible individuals were considered. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Pairwise Spearman&#x2019;s correlation coefficients over the same 1-Mb &lt;em&gt;EPSPS&lt;/em&gt; region for individuals shown in &lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;. Mean coefficient across all individuals within each agricultural region indicated. Order of individuals within each region as in &lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;, from &lt;em&gt;Bottom&lt;/em&gt; to &lt;em&gt;Top&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;Right&lt;/em&gt; to &lt;em&gt;Left&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F6.medium.gif" width="440" height="217"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 6." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F6.medium.gif" width="440" height="217"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/42/21076/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/893276" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 6.</span> <p id="p-28" class="first-child">Similarity of the <em>EPSPS</em> amplification inferred from copy number variation around the <em>EPSPS</em> gene. (<em>A</em>) The profile of normalized sequence coverage for 1 Mb around EPSPS (locus delimited by dashed line). Color of each bar under the curve represents copy number at that genomic position, where height of each profile has been rescaled by the max value within each region for clarity (Walpole, Essex, and Midwest, separately). Only individuals with at least 1.5× coverage relative to susceptible individuals were considered. (<em>B</em>) Pairwise Spearman’s correlation coefficients over the same 1-Mb <em>EPSPS</em> region for individuals shown in <em>A</em>. Mean coefficient across all individuals within each agricultural region indicated. Order of individuals within each region as in <em>A</em>, from <em>Bottom</em> to <em>Top</em> and <em>Right</em> to <em>Left</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-7"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-29" class="flushleft">The patterns of genetic differentiation and similarity in amplification profiles among agricultural regions helped us to distinguish between modes of adaptation, the evolutionary mechanisms by which glyphosate resistance has spread, and the extent of constraint on this particular genetic pathway. Although the Walpole population showed signs of admixture from var. <em>rudis</em>, Walpole individuals were clearly differentiated at <em>EPSPS</em> from both Essex and Midwest individuals (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>). Moreover, copy number profiles were almost perfectly correlated within Walpole, but showed low correlations with Essex and the Midwestern individuals (<a id="xref-fig-6-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>B</em></a>). This suggests that glyphosate resistance in Walpole evolved independently, likely from selection on a de novo amplification event, although we do not know whether the amplification occurred in Walpole, or whether this allele was introgressed from an unsampled population. In Essex, the lack of within-region correlation in <em>EPSPS</em> copy profiles and sporadic high correlations with individuals from different Midwestern populations (<a id="xref-fig-6-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6<em>B</em></a>), suggest multiple independent amplification events. Together with the lack of genetic differentiation between Essex and Midwest (both genome-wide and on all of chromosome 5, including EPSPS; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1900870116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>), this suggests that Essex was either directly colonized by a diverse glyphosate-resistant population from the Midwest, or that a prior glyphosate-susceptible population in Essex was replaced by glyphosate-resistant individuals from the Midwest.</p><p id="p-30">In summary, we have found multiple modes of convergent evolution underlying the spread of glyphosate resistance in North American <em>A. tuberculatus</em> populations. There is evidence for a single <em>EPSPS</em> amplification event that gave rise to the resistant populations in Walpole, distinct from amplification events in populations from another Canadian region, Essex County, and from populations in the US Midwest, where glyphosate resistance is older than in Canada. In contrast to the hard sweep in Walpole, glyphosate selection has left only soft selective sweep signals in the Midwest, because different haplotypes were amplified independently. Together with our analyses of population structure and demographic history, these results suggest that evolution on the more agriculturally naive, and recently bottlenecked <em>A. tuberculatus</em> var. <em>tuberculatus</em> background occurred in a mutation-limited framework, relying on evolutionary rescue via de novo mutation. In contrast, multiple independent amplification haplotypes have been maintained both within and among populations of <em>A. tuberculatus</em> var. <em>rudis</em>, likely resulting from its recent population expansion, long-range gene flow (as in Essex), and a longer history of spatially and temporally fluctuating selection [as suggested in Kreiner et al. (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>)]. Therefore, demographic history and duration of selection have interacted to determine whether adaptation remains constrained to a mutation-limited framework.</p><p id="p-31">A practical outcome of this work is that it informs on the scale of management that is needed to control herbicide resistance. Specifically, we suggest that with glyphosate resistance spreading across the range through seed translocation and independent adaptation, management efforts should be broadened to encompass both regional seed containment and local integrative control of herbicide-resistant weeds. We are faced with an additional challenge—that historically nonweedy lineages can adapt to an agricultural environment on rapid, contemporary timescales—calling for more consideration of how to prevent seemingly benign weeds from becoming problematic.</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Methods</h2><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Plant Collections.</h3><p id="p-32">Seeds were collected from Midwestern populations in 2010 (<a id="xref-ref-11-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), and from Ontario natural populations and agricultural fields in the fall of 2016. Agricultural fields in which <em>A. tuberculatus</em> appeared to be poorly controlled were sampled, biasing the collection toward populations with high levels of glyphosate resistance. These do not necessarily represent levels of resistance in a random sample.</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>High–Molecular-Weight DNA Extraction.</h3><p id="p-33">High–molecular-weight (HMW) DNA was extracted from the leaf tissue of a single 28-d-old female <em>A. tuberculatus</em> plant from the Midwest using a modified version of the Doyle and Doyle nuclei isolation protocol (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). Nuclei isolation was carried out by incubating 30 g of ground leaf tissue in a buffer comprising tris(hydroxymethyl)aminomethane, potassium chloride, EDTA, sucrose, spermidine, and spermine tetrahydrochloride (Sigma-Aldrich). The homogenate was subsequently filtered using miracloth and precipitated by centrifugation. G2 lysis buffer, RNase A, and Proteinase K (Qiagen) were then added prior to an overnight incubation at 50 °C, followed by centrifugation at 4 °C. The supernatant containing the DNA solution was added to an equilibrated Qiagen genomic tip 100 (Qiagen). Genomic DNA was eluted and precipitated using isopropanol. Finally, HMW DNA was isolated by spooling.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>SMRTbell Library Preparation and Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-34">HMW genomic DNA was sheared to 30 kb using a Megaruptor 2 instrument (Diagenode SA). DNA damage and end repair were carried out prior to blunt adaptor ligation and exonuclease purification using ExoIII and ExoVII, in accordance with the protocol supplied by Pacific Biosciences (P/N 101-024-600-02; Pacific Biosciences). The resultant SMRTbell templates were size-selected using a BluePippin (SageScience) instrument with a 15-kb cutoff and a 0.75% DF Marker S1 high-pass 15- to 20-kb gel cassette. The final library was sequenced on a Sequel System (Pacific Biosciences) with v2 chemistry, MagBead loading, and SMRT Link UI v4 analysis.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Lucigen PCR-Free Library Preparation and Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-35">Genomic DNA was fragmented to 350-bp size using a Covaris S2 Focused Ultrasonicator (Covaris). Subsequent end-repair, A-tailing, Lucigen adaptor ligation, and size selection were performed using the Lucigen NxSeq AMPFree Low DNA Library Kit (Lucigen). Libraries were quantified using a Qubit 2.0 instrument (Life Technologies), and library profiles were analyzed using a Bioanalyzer High Sensitivity Chip on an Agilent Bioanalyzer 2100 (Agilent Technologies). The libraries were sequenced to a coverage depth of 10× on an HiSeq 3000 instrument (Illumina) using a HiSeq 3000/4000 SBS kit and paired-end 150 base read chemistry. Raw fastq files were deposited to ENA (project no. PRJEB31711) (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Assembly and Haplotype Merging.</h3><p id="p-36">The genome was assembled from 58 Gb of Sequel long read data using <em>Canu</em> (version 1.6; genomeSize = 544 m; other parameters default) (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>). Raw contigs were polished with <em>Arrow</em> (ConsensusCore2, version 3.0.0; consensus models S/P2-C2 and S/P2-C2/5.0; other parameters default) and <em>Pilon</em> (version 1.22; parameters default) (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>). Polished contigs were repeat masked using <em>WindowMasker</em> (version 1.0.0; -checkdup; other parameters default) (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). Repeat-masked contigs were screened for misjoints and subjected to haplotype merging using <em>HaploMerger2</em> [commit 95f8589; identity = 80, other parameters default (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>)]. A custom scoring matrix was supplied to both <em>lastz</em> steps of <em>Haplomerger2</em> (misjoint and haplotype detection). The scoring matrix was inferred from an all-vs.-all contig alignment using <em>minimap2</em> (version 2.10; preset asm10; other parameters default) (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>) taking only the best contig-to-contig alignments into account. The final assembly was finished against the chromosome-resolved <em>A. hypochondriacus</em> genome (<a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>) using <em>reveal finish</em> (commit 98d3ad1; –fixedgapsize –gapsize 15,000; other parameters default) (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). The 16 resulting pseudo-chromosomes represented 99.6% of the original assembly.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Alignment, SNP Calling, and Gene Annotation.</h3><p id="p-37">We used <em>freebayes</em> (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>) to call SNPs jointly on all samples. For whole-genome analyses, we used a thoroughly filtered SNP set following established guidelines (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>, <a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>) adapted for whole-genome data: sites were removed based on missing data (&gt;80%), complexity, indels, allelic bias (&lt;0.25 and &gt;0.75), whether there was a discrepancy in paired status of reads supporting reference or alternate alleles, and mapping quality (QUAL &lt; 30, representing sites with greater than a 1/1,000 error rate). Individuals with excess missing data (&gt;5%) were dropped. This led to a final, high-confidence SNP set of 10,280,132 sites. For <em>EPSPS</em>-specific analyses and genome-wide investigations that required invariant sites, we recalled SNPs with <em>samtools</em> (V1.7; ref. <a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>) and <em>bwa-mem</em> (V0.7.17; ref. <a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). For this SNP set, sites were minimally filtered on mapping quality and missing data (keeping only sites with MQ &gt;30 and &lt;20% missing data), so that diversity estimates were not biased by preferentially retaining invariant or variant sites. For both SNP sets, we used <em>bwa-mem</em> to map to our fastqs to the reference genome. Bam files were sorted and duplicates marked with <em>sambamba</em> (V0.6.6; ref. <a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>), while cigars were split and read groups added with <em>picard</em> (V2.17.11).</p><p id="p-38">We performed gene annotation on both our final assembly and the <em>A. hypochondriacus</em>-finished pseudoassembly using the <em>MAKER</em> pipeline (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). <em>A. tuberculatus</em>-specific repeats were identified using <em>RepeatModeler</em> (v1.0.11; ref. <a id="xref-ref-13-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>), combined with the RepBase repeat library, and masked with <em>RepeatMasker</em> (v4.0.7; ref. <a id="xref-ref-14-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>). This repeat-masked genome was then run through <em>MAKER</em> (v2.31.8), using expressed sequence tag evidence from an <em>A. tuberculatus</em> transcriptome assembly (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>) and protein homology evidence from <em>A. hypochondriacus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>). The gene models were further annotated using <em>InterProScan</em> (v69.0; ref. <a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>), resulting in a total of 30,771 genes and 40,766 transcripts with a mean transcript length of 1,245 bp. The mean annotation edit distance (AED) score was 0.21, and 98.1% of the gene predictions had an AED score of &lt;0.5, indicating high-quality annotations.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Phenotyping.</h3><p id="p-39">Seedlings from each population were grown in a 1:1:1:1 soil:peat:Torpedo Sand:LC1 (SunGro commercial potting mix) medium supplemented with 13-13-13 Osmocote in a greenhouse that was maintained at 28/22 °C day/night temperatures for a 16:8-h photoperiod. Plants were sprayed at the 5 to 7 leaf stage with 1,260 g of glyphosate (WeatherMax 4.5 L; Monsanto) per hectare. Fourteen days after treatment, plants were rated visually on a scale of 0 (highly sensitive) to 5 (no injury). Plants rated 2 or higher were classified as resistant. Prior to herbicide treatment, single leaf samples were taken from each plant and stored at −80 °C until ready for genomic DNA extraction. Tissue from plants rated as highly glyphosate resistant or susceptible were selected from each population for genomic DNA extraction using a modified cetrimonium bromide method (<a id="xref-ref-41-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Copy Number Estimates.</h3><p id="p-40">Scaled coverage and copy number at <em>EPSPS</em> was estimated by dividing the coverage at each site across the focal region by the mode of genome-wide coverage after excluding centromeric regions (which have repeats and thus often abnormally high coverage) and regions of low coverage (&lt;3×, indicative of technical coverage bias), which should represent the coverage of single-copy genes.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Structure, Demographic Modeling, and Summary Statistics.</h3><p id="p-41">To model neutral demographic history and estimate neutral diversity, we used a Python script (available at <a href="https://github.com/tvkent/Degeneracy" class="in-nw">https://github.com/tvkent/Degeneracy</a>) to score 0-fold and 4-fold degenerate sites across the genome. This procedure estimated 17,454,116 0-fold and 4,316,850 4-fold sites across the genome, and after intersecting with our final high-quality <em>freebayes</em>-called SNP set, resulted in 345,543 0-fold SNPs and 326,459 4-fold SNPs. The latter was used as input for demographic modeling.</p><p id="p-42">Our 2-population demographic model of <em>A. tuberculatus</em> modeled the split between the <em>A. tuberculatus</em> var. <em>tuberculatus</em> and var. <em>rudis</em> subspecies by collapsing individuals into 1 of the 2 populations based on predominant ancestry as identified in our <em>STRUCTURE</em> analyses, estimated in ∂a∂i (V1.7.0) (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>) using the pipeline available on <a href="https://github.com/dportik/dadi_pipeline" class="in-nw">https://github.com/dportik/dadi_pipeline</a> (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>). 1D and 2D site frequency spectrums were estimated using the program <em>easySFS</em> (<a href="https://github.com/isaacovercast/easySFS" class="in-nw">https://github.com/isaacovercast/easySFS</a>), and samples were projected downward to maximize the number of loci without missing data vs. number of individuals retained. We ensured that the log-likelihood of our parameter set had optimized by iterating the analysis over 4 rounds of increasing reps, from 10 to 40. We tested a set of 20 diversification models, with variation in split times, symmetry of migration, constancy of migration, population sizes, and size changes. The most likely inferred demography followed a model of secondary contact, where initially populations split without gene flow, followed by population size change with asymmetrical gene flow, and included 8 parameters: size of population 1 after split (nu1a), size of population 2 after split (nu2a), the scaled time between the split and the secondary contact (in units of 2*N<sub>a</sub> generations) (T1), the scaled time between the secondary contact and present (T<sub>2</sub>), size of population 1 after time interval (nu1b), size of population 2 after time interval (nu2b), migration from population 2 to population 1 (2*N<sub>a</sub>*m12), and migration from population 1 to population 2 (m21). N<sub>e</sub> was calculated by substituting the per-site θ estimate (after controlling for the effective sequence length to account for losses in the alignment and missed or filtered calls) and the Arabidopsis <em>thaliana</em> mutation rate (7 × 10<sup>−9</sup>) (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">61</a>) into the equation <span class="inline-formula" id="inline-formula-1"><span class="mathjax mml-math"><mml:math xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><mml:mrow><mml:mi>θ</mml:mi><mml:mo>=</mml:mo><mml:mn>4</mml:mn><mml:mi>N</mml:mi><mml:mi>e</mml:mi><mml:mi>μ</mml:mi></mml:mrow></mml:math></span></span>.</p><p id="p-43">We used <em>PLINK</em> (V1.9; ref. <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>) to perform a PCA of genotypes from our final <em>freebayes</em> SNP set after thinning to reduce the effects of sites that are in linkage disequilibrium, used STRUCTURE (V2.3.4) (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>) to estimate admixture across populations, and <em>treemix</em> (V3) (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>) to infer patterns of population splitting and migration events. To calculate summary statistics (π, F<sub>ST</sub>, <em>D</em><sub><em>xy</em></sub>), we used scripts from the genomics general pipeline available at <a href="https://github.com/simonhmartin/genomics_general" class="in-nw">https://github.com/simonhmartin/genomics_general</a>, binning SNPs into 100-kb windows with a step size of 10 kb. To estimate the proportion of introgression of var. <em>rudis</em> ancestry into Walpole agricultural populations in these genomic windows, we used the <em>f</em> statistic (but with nonoverlapping windows) (<a id="xref-ref-28-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). For investigation of introgression of Natural populations into Illinois (var. <em>tuberculatus</em> into var. <em>rudis</em>), we used Missouri as the reference ingroup. For investigation of introgression of Essex populations into Walpole (var. <em>tuberculatus</em> into var. <em>rudis</em>), we used Natural populations as the reference ingroup. Last, for investigation of introgression of Natural populations into Essex (var. <em>tuberculatus</em> into var. <em>rudis</em>), we used Missouri as the reference ingroup. To get CIs for the <em>f</em> statistic estimates, we performed jackknifing by calculating pseudovalues by removing one 250-kb block at a time.</p><p id="p-44">For the outlier analysis of putative genes underlying contemporary agricultural adaptation in Walpole, we analyzed genome-wide differentiation (F<sub>st</sub>) in 10-kb windows, and classified windows as outliers when they were in the top 1% for extreme differentiation. A GO enrichment test was then performed for these outlier regions, after finding their intersecting annotated <em>A. tuberculatus</em> genes, and their orthologs in <em>A. thaliana</em> using <em>orthofinder</em> (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">65</a>). To look at the possibility of gene expansion in these enriched gene families, we first characterized normalized copy number in 100-bp windows within each annotated gene in that family, for every individual. We then characterized the median copy number across windows within each gene for each individual, as heterogeneous mapping of paralogs/orthologs due to differential levels of degeneration should lead to variation in copy number across windows within the gene. We then compared the distribution of the median copy number between Walpole and Natural population individuals for every gene. We tested whether the distribution of median copy number of each gene differed consistently across all genes and between Walpole and Natural populations by performing an ANOVA of region and gene ID, and allowing for an interaction. Scripts and code are available at <a href="https://github.com/jkreinz/Amaranthus-population-genomics" class="in-nw">https://github.com/jkreinz/Amaranthus-population-genomics</a> (<a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Detecting Selective Sweeps and Estimating Recombination Rate.</h3><p id="p-45">To detect differences in the strength and breadth of sweep signal associated with selection from glyphosate across geographic regions, we used SNPs called from the pseudoassembly of our <em>A. tuberculatus</em> reference. Sweep detection can be strongly influenced by heterogeneity in recombination rate, and so as a control (in our <em>Sweepfinder2</em> and <em>XPEHH</em> analyses), we used the interval function in <em>LDhat</em> (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">67</a>) to estimate variable recombination rate independently across all 16 chromosomes of the pseudoassembly, using a precomputed lookup table for a θ of 0.01 for 192 chromosomes. Accordingly, we randomly subsetted individuals to retain only 96 individuals for computation of recombination rate estimates, which was implemented by segmenting the genome into 2,000 SNP windows, following the workflow outline in <a href="https://github.com/QuentinRougemont/LDhat_workflow" class="in-nw">https://github.com/QuentinRougemont/LDhat_workflow</a>.</p><p id="p-46">To account for the fact that high-copy number loci may allow for increased diversity relative to single-copy regions, we randomly sampled 1 allele per locus along the length of chromosome 5 to create pseudohaploid haplotypes for our sweep scans. This ensures that any increased differentiation is due to differences among individuals, rather than among haplotypes within individuals. The <em>XP-EHH</em> scan (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>), calculated based on the difference in haplotype homozygosity between amplified and nonamplified individuals for each geographic region after controlling for recombination rate, was implemented in <em>selscan</em> (<a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a>). Scripts available at <a href="https://github.com/simonhmartin/genomics_general" class="in-nw">https://github.com/simonhmartin/genomics_general</a> were used for calculating differentiation and the difference in diversity. Pseudohaploid haplotypes were also used to calculate a maximum-likelihood tree for the 235 SNPs in <em>EPSPS</em>. For each tree, we realigned sequences before bootstrapping 1,000 replicates of our haplotree with <em>clustal omega</em> (<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>). In contrast to haplotype-based methods that required phased data, we also ran <em>Sweepfinder2</em> (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>, <a id="xref-ref-34-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>), a program that compares the likelihood of a selective skew in the site frequency spectrum (SFS) at focal windows compared to the background SFS while controlling for heterogeneity in recombination rate. The SFSs of 10-kb windows across chromosome 5 were compared to the genome-wide SFSs at 4-fold degenerate sites, that for this analysis, was also randomly sampled for 1 allele per locus, for an equivalent comparison of the SFS. Last, we investigated similarity in the EPSPS amplification within and among populations and regions by estimating the Spearman’s rank correlation coefficient for all pairwise comparisons of resistant, amplification-containing individuals. This was done for the 1-Mb region surrounding EPSPS.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-47">We thank Tyler Kent and Anna O’Brien for useful discussion; Rebecca Schwab, Fernando Rabanal, and Talia Karasov for comments; and Yunchen Gong for computational support. We also thank and recognize the Ojibwe, Potawatomi, and Odawa peoples of the Walpole Island First Nation.         <div class="node--featured-item__description text-small">Amanda Rodewald, Ivan Rudik, and Catherine Kling talk about the hazards of ozone pollution to birds.</div>
    

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__listen"><a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="button-alt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Listen</a></div>
    <div class="node--featured-item__links"><span><a href="http://feeds.feedburner.com/pnas/uSDM" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Past Podcasts</a></span><span><a href="https://itunes.apple.com/us/podcast/pnas-science-sessions/id1367460245" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Subscribe</a></span></div>
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-odd views-row-last">
    <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/39/24195" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/pitn-1-01-11-220.jpg?itok=uHvXa70H" width="220" height="220" alt="Goats standing in a pin" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/39/24195" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Transplantation of sperm-producing stem cells</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing can improve the effectiveness of spermatogonial stem cell transplantation in mice and livestock, a study finds.</div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__credits text-small">Image credit: Jon M. Oatley.</div>
